		<shortDescription>good performances, implausible story line; see it anyway</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>spoilers- story outline and plot ploy revealedI liked this flick because the actors do a good job. The story is simple; a middle aged neurotic con man who lives alone goes for the long con or a big money prize while he trys to cope with the appearance of his never-seen-before 14 year old daughter in his otherwise neurotically ordered life. He's neurotic about cleanliness, bright sunshine and insists on opening and closing the doors 3 times before he finally swings the door back. Funny thing is he only does this in private and never with car doors or shop doors etc. Anyhow aside from being implausible in this matter the film also suggests that the man's lack of an emotional relationship is at the base of his neurosis. Hey presto get a relationship and he's not-neurotic and enjoys life for a change rather than earning a big pile of money as he's being going along doing so far. I suppose it's the magic of hollywood but I couldn't help feeling that this idea is the oldest con known to entertainers, but not just any old entertainers hollywood ones in particular. Aside from this oldest trick in the 2nd oldest trade this is a good film worth a look, Alison Lohman the 14 year old is suitably engaging to win over an audience to the idea she could win over her emotional basket case 'splitup with her mom from birth' streetwise con man dad. Nicholas Cage who I am no fan of plays this kind of role well, and the switch back to a so called functional dude works well, he even gets some laughs as his orderly life goes 'off the rails'. The subdued approach of the film by blockbuster director Ridley Scott is good but I wouldn't rent the movie if I were a fan of his but I did rent it knowing a capable director was behind the package. All in all entertaining but a little self mockery at the con men of con men that is hollywood, taking the viewer for a ride past the same place for the thousandth time like some 2nd rate cheap crook taxi driver would have made this film better and more realistic for the ounce of honesty it'd have introduced. This may sound like schadenfreude but it's really a plea for artistic responsibility of sorts. 7.4 out of 10 Viva Hollywood!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>These comments are intended for people who have already seen the movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>spoilers There are two ways of looking at MM. To talk about it as a con movie involves divulging the plot, which should be left for the viewers' own enjoyment. I am therefore more inclined to look at it as a father-and-daughter movie. With two sons but no daughter, it's easier for me to empathize with movies such as Johnny Q. I do of course enjoy movies such as I Am Sam. While there are many reasonably good movies in this category, the best is Poppoya (1999) (Railroad Man), which I found deeply moving. There are also movies which, although technical not father-and-daughter, have the similar sentiments. Of these, the best is Whale Rider (2002) (grandfather and granddaughter). And then, there are other movies where father-and-daughter type of sentiments are present, although not the main theme. A most prominent, recent example is Theoden and Eowyn, in Return of the King. Even in Mystic River, we see a little bit of sweet father-and-daughter relationship before the daughter is murdered. The unique thing about the father-daughter relationship in MM is the layering. But even before the layering develops, when the daughter (portrayed Alison Lohman) first shows up, there is immediately a breath of freshness that is perhaps a little unexpected. The easy congeniality with which she handles this first encounter not only calms the sometimes neurotic father (Nick Cage), but also leaves the audience with a sense of relief that they are spared a sentimental scene of cliché. While there is more than meets the eye, the audience fully enjoys the ride as the relationship deepens and takes some familiar turns. This second layer works so well that the final scene, on which the entire film stands or falls, becomes convincing. Cage's line `I know your name' says it all, while the girls parting acknowledgement of `dad' has a natural sincerity. The final shot, a mere glimpse of the girl's gaiety in the car, effectively conveys the message that a considerably heavy burden had been taken off her back as a result of this chance meeting. Cage and Lohman deserve a lot of credit in making this whole thing work. As to the con part, the audience is given two good hints. In the father's first lesson to the daughter, he gives a cardinal rule: don't be conned by the people you are trying to con. Then, in discussing upcoming projects with his partner, he says that he does not like `long cons'. Both these remarks become self-filling prophesies. One other thing I like about this movie is the liberal and skilful use of popular music from the sixties and seventies, and even earlier. While examples are abundant, the best is the Swedish Rhapsody used in the scenes when Cage is frantically cleaning and tidying up his house.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>does not live up to its potential</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The sad thing about this movie is how close it comes to greatness. The premise is terrific - an OCD, twitching, neurotic con man who gets reunited with his daughter and struggles to balance his quirks, his "job" and business partner, and his new relationship.You have SO much potential here! You can summon all those feelings of Leon (aka The Professional) and play on the hilarity that Cage provokes in a similar role in Adaptation. Even still, you can pull a terrific "gotcha" at the end as this film does and not be accused of deus ex machina (see Memento, Rosemary's Baby, American Psycho, Chinatown). What keeps this film from the greatness of the aforementioneds is not the performances, not the directing, not the cinematography - it's the writing! This film doesn't know if it's a comedic action film, a heart-felt drama, a black comedy, but it thinks it can be all three when it clearly can only pick one path. If only the screenwriters had invested more time in making this about the characters themselves and less about standard Hollywood plot formulas, you'd have a terrific film. Instead, it's just mediocre with a few good moments. Skip it!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A great cast in an ill-conceived effort</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Seeing Nicolas Cage explore a character with an Obsessive-Compulsive Disorder has an entertainment value that transcends any plot, good or bad.But when you decide to make a grifter the protagonist of your film, you are faced with a choice: You can make a "light" picture that entertains with the layers and intricacies of a "long con" (as James Foley does in 2003's "Confidence").Or you can resolve to do a genuine character study, as Stephen Frears did in "The Grifters" (where J.T. Walsh also gives a great performance and reveals the emotional tightrope that con artists walk).But in this movie, Ridley Scott attempts to navigate between those two extremes, and the result is a film where the audience is either unsure if they should sympathize with Cage's character, or they do sympathize, but feel manipulated.In presenting a character with terrible mental problem, Scott lays an "instant sympathy" card on the audience, only to yank the heartstrings in another direction with how the man makes a living. The result is confusion and a conflict that is never given a satisfactory resolution.The film is predictable as well, lacking the entertaining twists of other "heist" or "con" movies. And its a shame, because the film could have worked as a "light" piece with about 3 times more twists and turns.But when you place an actor like Cage in the role of a troubled person, the audience isn't going to "suspend reality" and simply enjoy the ride. Unfortunately, the "reality" that Scott then presents is wanting in many ways. I walked away from the film glad to have seen Cage do some hooting and twitching, but I sure didn't learn anything.</fullReview>

		<username>mppullar</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Ridley, what's going on? (In a good way)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I should start off by saying that I don't like Ridley Scott. I have admittedly only seen the German dubbed version of "1492" while overseas, and my German wasn't really good enough to appreciate it fully - but, while it was visually impressive, it didn't seem to be much more than a self-important, grandiose epic. My understanding is that many of Ridley's films fall into this trap. There are exceptions, I know - "Blade Runner" is supposed to be a masterpiece, and I'm not sure why I haven't seen it. But, as a rule, I just have no desire to see Russell Crowe play Gluteus Maximus, or watch Josh Hartnett be wasted in yet another Jerry Bruckheimer explosion-feast.SO...when I saw that Nicolas Cage, Sam Rockwell and Alison Lohmann, all actors that I love, were going to be in the new Ridley Scott film, I was certainly not excited - verging on despair would be a more accurate description. But then the reviews began to come in, and I was forced to reconsider what I expected. It sounded as if, for whatever reason, Ridley had decided to take a break from his usual films to make something much more intimate, taut and touching - it almost sounded as if "Matchstick Men" would be worth checking out.So yesterday I watched it. And I have to say that it was one of the best films of the year. Packed to the brim with surprises (but which, unlike in films like "Boxing Helena" or "Wild Things", were genuinely clever, genuinely suspenseful), with some stunning cinematography, clever use of editing, music, set design, even costume, and a truly touching (not at all sentimental or cloying) script, "Matchstick Men" is already streets ahead of most mainstream films to hit our cinemas at the moment. Add to that the sheer genius of Cage, Rockwell and Lohmann, all giving performances that rate among their finest, and you get a surprising, memorable and provocative film. If it were only a crime caper film, it would be much more tense and intelligent than most. If it were only an emotional drama, it would beat almost every other film of its kind for avoiding heavy-handed sentiment and genuinely sticking in your head and heart. Put these two together and you get a film that really SHOULDN'T have worked (who ever thought of putting "White Oleander" and "Nikita" together?), but it DOES work - it works marvellously. Perhaps Scott's style of direction could be a little more experimental, but it does have moments of fascinating experimentation which (again) put it ahead of any other film of its kind. And really, you just have to respect it for working. Don't be a genre purist when watching this film; and don't be cynically hardened to the possibility of it affecting you emotionally. If you do either of these, you will be unable to enjoy it. Let it be what it is, and respect it for doing what it does so well. And let it touch you, because it will if you allow it. I can't stop thinking about the relationships in this film, about Lohmann's beautiful performance, Cage's surprising restraint, and Rockwell's incredible sense of style. This film was beautiful - it really was. Four and a half out of five. (Excellent work, Ridley. But I'm still not going to watch the rest of your work.)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>In a rare surprise this year I found myself completely gratified with not only my experience watching `Matchstick Men' but as I walked out at the end thinking about it long afterwards. And it's due to the way the film was handled in a very professional manner by all involved that grabbed my attention. The story though was easy to absorb. The filmmakers approached this one with just the right amount of daring to make it interesting but with a small pinch of unadorned exposition and character development to remain steady and to the point.The tale of Roy Waller the con man is at the core of this film but it's the story of his deeply troubled life and the changes he undergoes that takes center stage, pushing the con-theme to one side. As you would imagine `Matchstick Men' is a smaller character driven film with only a handful of main characters without any elaborate designs or effects. This is a departure for Ridley Scott of late but it's a great example of just how crafty a director he really is. He knows precisely how to put together and structure a movie, not just as a story but also as an art form. With this film there is a pronounced sense of sincerity not to go overboard and just tell it the way it needs to be told, nothing more.`Matchstick Men' is a combination of genres so it's hard to categorise. I believe that everyone can find something here to enjoy because it's both exciting and intelligent without being too obvious about it. If you get the opportunity to see it then do so, trust me it's a few hours well worth it.Score - 4 out of 5</fullReview>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Overrated (**)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ridley Scott's "Matchstick Men" is unquestionably a very well-acted film. But well made? At times. At other times, not so much. Nicolas Cage gives a phenomenal performance as Roy Waller, a con man who suffers from a laundry list of psychological ailments. He has severe obssesive-compulsive disorder (for instance, he has to open a door 3 times before going through it) and is agoraphobic and prone to panic attacks. With all these problems, I have no idea how he manages to be a successful con man, but since Cage's performance is so entertaining and sympathetic, I didn't mind. Also entertaining as always is Sam Rockwell as Frank, Roy's considerably more slovenly partner in crime. Right as Roy and Frank are about to pull a big con, Roy's life is complicated by the arrival of his estranged 14 year-old daughter, Angela (Alison Lohman) who he has never gotten to know. Angela is an inquisitive tomboy, and before long is demanding to be part of Roy and Frank's escapades. The problem with this movie is the story. There's nothing wrong with the basics that I mentioned above, but rather the fact that this is yet another one of those movies where there's a Big Twist Ending that changes the whole rest of the movie. Those kinds of movies can be great, but here I knew where it was going the whole time, and didn't LIKE where I knew it was going. And when my suspicions were revealed to be true, it was done in a way that I found weak and unimaginative. Further complicating matters is a "One year later..." scene that allows for one of the flattest endings I've seen in a while. Maybe I'm sentimental, but sometimes I have to dislike a movie just because what I thought SHOULD happen, didn't. Once again, there's nothing wrong with the performances and the cinematography is pretty nice. I just didn't dig it.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Awesome. Movie making as art. Excellent sound work. A 9, but possibly a 10 if you include the parts that went over my head.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is great, no argument possible. Ridley Scott knows exactly what he is doing and it's no fluke. He's consistant. This movie shows what it means to make a movie into a work of art. Every element of this movie is great: plot, characters, motivations, script, dialogue, music, sound, editing....and on and on.The sound track is very detailed. I haven't noticed that before in many movies. Additional sounds like the chime of the seat belt warning, the sound of shoes on carpet, security chain on the door ratteling, the detail is amazing and enjoyable and surprising.The music is excellent. Ranging from a 13 year old Wayne Newton singing "Danka Shern" to an alternate version of "More Than This" (made famous by 10,000 Maniacs) the music is again, detailed, enjoyable, and surprising. Also, the music intrudes on the movie just enough. Enough to notice and augment the action, but not enough to become a distraction.The editing and direction are excellent and done in a way that is "art" rather than simply "getting the job done." During the time when Roy was waiting for Angela at the park, there was a rapid fire succession of images. My first thought was it was like a computer program, following a logical and methodical process at lightning speed to deliver the intended effect. Not bad, but, hmmm, not exactly. Other thoughts I had about this scene were collage, montage, music video, mosaic, or painting. I'm not sure exactly how to describe it, but it was incredible and I appreciate this kind of effort in today's movie making climate.I give this movie a 9 because it was excellent, but it did not have the inspiring effect on me that I require to award a 10. However, I realized that some, possibly much, of the artistry and content of the movie went over my head. If I had understood it all, I would probably have to award a 10.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-10-16</date>
		<username>Sinnerman</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Somebody give Nic his second Oscar already!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>My rating: **** out of ****Today is great day for me, as something joyous happened. I have just seen Matchstick Men.Here is a descriptive trajectory of my myriad reaction to this flick. By the first quarter I knew I would like the film. By the second quarter I loved it to bits. By the third though, I had an about-turn and hated hated hated it. But in the end, it closed on such a right note, I knew there and then that nothing less than a four star rating is deserving of this immensely insightful film. I am thus very hard pressed as to how best I should tackle a review for this flick, for its the kinda film that needs to be experienced and not read about. Of course, I cannot detail the reasons behind above rollercoaster ride, without revealing some of its major "sights". So how? The Sinnerman is physically incapable of churning out short takes? How?Then it hit me. I need not write about the film, I just need to share with all what I felt about it, what I learnt from it, and what's my "experience" of it. Well, the one overriding experience I'm bursting to share is the one powerhouse performance of one Nicholas Cage.So here goes....Nic was a borderline psycho who suffered from a potent mix of agoraphobia (fear of open spaces) and an obsessive-compulsive fixation with hygiene; he literally needed things clean. In fact, the guy spent an early part of the film ceaselessly cleaning and sanitising his entire apartment, for he accidentally thrown out his "medication". Oh, did I mention the guy was also a professional con artist, and a very good one at that? In fact, the whole film saw him planning on this huge scam with his partner (Sam Rockwell)? On top of this, he was also concurrently getting in touch with his paternal instincts, via a recent reunion with his teenage daughter after 14 years?Like a tug of war for the heart &amp; mind, Nic's character was hence struggling to stay afloat. His hands were obviously full from juggling so many challenges on so many fronts (work and home, body and spirit, love and relationships etc). On the outset, this already sounded like the kinda un-subtle characters that Cage seemed stereotypically gravitated towards these last few years. But the reason for this is that few actors can navigate equal arts madness and sensitivity the way he can. My only regret in fact, was that Spike Jonze never considered a "Being Nicholas Cage".Regardless, for I do think that both this performance and his last (Adaptation) has finally hark back to his Leaving Las Vegas days (IMO, still his career's best performance). For just underneathe the veneer of his OTT shtick, laid an evocative gulf of pain &amp; vulnerability, emotional neediness &amp; ironically, a generous dose of decency and goodness.Above uncomfortable mix of psychological markers are never easy to articulate individually, much less synergistically. It is thus a measure of Cage's certified acting chops that he imperceptibly blended it all together and made it so easy for us to identify them all on screen. His lucid performance in this unforgettable role was thus so spot-on, it cut through my skin, flesh and bone and back out the flesh and skin. Such experiential decapitation is becoming less frequent, the more movies I see. I am thus wildly appreciative (grateful even) for those all-round extra efforts taken. Somebody give Cage his second Oscar already!Okay, let me explain further why I felt Nic Cage's character deserved my unprecedented, undivided attention for this whole thought piece. For it exhibited an uncanny intuition for anyone who's ever been afflicted by psychological "wounds" before. It attempted to shed light on why some people act they way they do. Most of all, it aimed to convey something we all ought to listen and learn about.We have all seen various shades of people like Cage's character before. You know, the kind who's very capable on their professional, intellectual front but fail terribly in all other departments, from arrested emotional developments to social situational blindness to all round lack of relationship intelligence. These people are, for lack of a better word, embarrassing.But Cage struck such a perfect note as "the conduit" of this film; he successfully channeled the film's condemnation for insensitive pricks like me. For he revealed hidden depths in these "caricatures" which I seldom (care to) think about. Despite all their norm-aversed idiosyncrasies, these people may too have pain with no one to share with, they may too have fears that can't be allayed and they may too have love but no one/ nowhere to give? We are all conflicted people, us humans. But not being socially "malleable" may serve to make one even more vulnerable to such weaknesses. The lack of social support (from friends and families) may amplify one's vulnerabilities.But being humans, I'll like to think that we are all still capable of virtues like decency, patience, generosity, love and all other variation of basic goodness, in spite of our personality failures. Cage hence capably conveyed these heartening reflections in Matchstick Men. He did not expect sympathies from us. He was more than capable of working out his issues. I though, the voyeur of this flick, should wise up to one thing; the offensiveness of my very act in dishing out unnecessary sympathies. What a positive spin that was? What a presumptuous b**tard I had been made out to be? I am much humbled indeed.In closing, it must be acknowledged that none of the above revelations would have been possible, if not for the seering clarity and boundlessly fearless performance by Nicholas Cage. I instinctively responds to such forceful deliveries. He thus deserves every bit of my OTT trumpeting. So you don't believe me, do you? This drool gooped piece of Cage worship isn't worth it, is it? Whatever. Just go see this film and let's talk some more.I'm done. For now. Later.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>House of Games meets Paper Moon meets As Good as It Gets</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*** Spoiler (?) *** I wonder if David Mamet was ever asked permission for being so heavily "quoted" by Scott in this movie. The core of MM is very much similar to Mamet's "The House of Games".The sequence when Cage and Rockwell talk outside the car after the big sting gone awry is almost identical to the one where Mantegna and his buddy stage a similar conversation in Mamet's film. The only thing that's different is the identity of the victim.... Apart from that, Cage is brilliantly playing a smalltime con with a load of neurosis and tics in desperate need of affection. Much in the same vein of Nicholson in "As Good As it Gets". When he meets with is long lost daughter the chemistry between them mirrors the one between the two O' Neals in "Paper Moon". The performances are consistently good and the writing is impeccable, the only - minor - flaw is a kind of patchwork sensation, if you get my drift. The film mixes many atmospheres not to say genres. It's not a comedy, not a thriller, not a drama, but a medley of all these. Luckily, it works. Just one more thing: Sam Rockwell. Great performance.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hard to review this film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERSTruth be told, I think I probably summed up this film with my title. "Matchstick Men" is at times powerful, at times moving, yet at times slow and tedious. Nicolas Cage is occasionally stunning as Roy Waller, a conman with Obsessive Compulsive Disorder, but at times his performance is similar to the disorder itself and is incredibly inconsistent. Cage seems to switch from stunning acting one moment, to ridiculously wooden the next. His character provides the occasional laugh, but sadly too many laughs are provided by his closing of doors. When studying Obsessive Compulsive Disorder, an actor should watch "As Good As It Gets" and witness Jack Nicholson's performance to view how to treat it properly. Sadly Cage obviously didn't.Other than Cage, the acting is relatively upto scratch but nothing special. Sam Rockwell as Frank Mercer is playing what seems like exactly the same character as he always plays. I wouldn't say Rockwell gets a bit type cast, simply as he isn't, but in everything I've seen him in, he's got different characters and mingled them to make the same person.Irrelevant of acting capabilities, what else can be said about this film? The plot is up to scratch, if somewhat predictable at times (although one twist at the end is clever, if obvious looking back at it), the directing is as you'd expect for Ridley Scott &amp; the cinematography is at times stunning. Other than this, there is relatively little to say. This film is by no means the best film of the year, but it is by no means the worst. It's good to watch if nothing else is available and you fancy to be moved (and slightly depressed perhaps), but ultimately, don't expect anything special.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Simply a great film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's rare in this day and age we get a good story well told, without having to resort to huge explosions, CGI or large breasted eye candy. Matchstick Men is one of these rarities.From start to finish, the intricate layer of personalities and storylines is rich and diverse, even the minor characters are fleshed-out and convincing. The Story is very strong, as one would expect from a novel adaptation, and the viewer isn't allowed a moment's rest.Cage's portrayal of someone suffering from OCD is very accurate, he has to open and close doors three times, he becomes obsessed over dirt and dust etc. If you've ever known someone with this disorder I'm sure you'll see a little of them in Cage's character.I liked the way that his various tics rise and fall in intensity based upon how you would expect the character to be feeling at that point of the movi e. When he feels bad, the tics are worse, and vice versa. I think is a very clever use of symbolism, and helps to give a deeper understanding of what's going on in his mind.Cage plays a conman with integrity and moral fibre so poor, it's as if he's made of matchsticks. When he suddenly finds himself having to raise a young girl, he finds himself having to examine the example he's setting for her and the effects he is going to have on her life. It's a charming, well told story which is both funny and thoughtful.I'd have liked to have seen more of Gary Oldman, Cage's long suffering partner in crime, the dynamic between these two Hollywood giants was rather good, and left me wanting to see more of them together.I won't talk about the ending, as so many others have done.I have a message for all the morons who felt it necessary to talk about the end though:You either fall into one of the following two categories.1: Yes, well done, you managed to sit through a whole movie, and OH MY GOSH, it didn't turn out exactly how you expected. It happens, get over it.2: Waaay to go, you predicted something vaguely in the region of what would happen, you're some kind of super genius at spotting movie plotlines, I bet your friends simply LOVE the way you point out what's going to happen in a loud voice 5 minutes before it does. (or they would if you had any) And now you're off to post how awesome you are on the internet. Could you BE any more pathetic?In both cases, please don't spoil it for others who haven't seen it by giving away the surprise. I know most of you don't do it on purpose, but giving away any information at all about a films ending, no matter how trivial you think it is, is ruining it for someone. Keep to a common rule, No talking about the end of films ever.</fullReview>

		<username>David A Dein</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Like A Big Joke!! But still great!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Have you ever had a really great prank played on you? No matter how funny the prank actually is, its been played at your expense and so you laugh heartily, but inside you're pretty p***ed off? That's how I felt sitting through Ridley Scott's MATCHSTICK MAN. A film that's so good, and yet in the end, goes one step too far.The story stars Nicholas Cage (Adaptation) as Roy Waller, a con-artist with what seems like OCD. He can't help but clean things; he a tad bit crazy that way. His partner in crime is Frank (Sam Rockwell, Confessions of a Dangerous Mind); he's the brawn of the operation to Roy's brains. Frank spends most of the movie setting up people, and Roy goes in for the kill.When Roy runs out of his medication, he goes to a shrink to get some more. Only to face the fact that he wishes he had a relationship with his daughter (Alison Lohman, White Oleander). Eventually the two meet and they strike up a relationship. Pretty soon she's in on some of Roy and Frank's con jobs.MATCHSTICK MEN is a brilliantly conceived movie that refuses to remain at all conventional. Roy and his daughter Angela have this unique and fun relationship. It's great to watch a man fight his quirks for the people he loves. I loved that the moment Angela shows up on Roy's doorstep that his immaculate house is dirtied and yet Roy doesn't seem bothered by it. That's real progress.Nicholas Cage does a fine job as Roy. He walks the thin line between a real person and a cartoon so well, you sometimes forget you're looking at the guy from SNAKE EYES. He plays these larger than life characters with so much vigor you forget he's acting.Cage is best in the small scenes in the Supermarket. There is this beautiful women (Shelia Kelley, Nurse Betty) that Roy really loves. They have this connection with so few words. Like they long to spill their life stories out at each other, but they just say hi to each other and that's almost enough. When Roy finally asks her name it's like a floodgate has open, and he is a man reborn.What bugs me about this film is that I'm not sure about the ending. It's everything I have ever wanted in a movie and yet it left me cold. It's hard to explain why because it would ruin the movie. But I guess it's an important lesson in the fact that "there is no such thing as a free lunch", and "what you sow, must also you reap."Now that I think of it, this film reminds me so much of the outcome of A CLOCKWORK ORANGE. Of course Alex deserved what he got in the end, but don't you feel sorry about it. Roy is not a good person, he's taken advantage of everyone who he's come into contact with, but can loving one person be enough penance for a lifetime of sins?All in all Ridley Scott sets a nice light tone on what otherwise could have been a more standard gritty and dramatic picture. His visual style is really close to Noir without the faded fedoras and the stanch seriousness. It's definitely one of those films that will have a hard time being jammed into just one genre category, and that's okay with me.Why not give MATCHSTICK MEN a look, you may be left scratching your head in the end, but at least it's a compelling ride.**** out of 5</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Matchstick men is one of those films that resurects a genre while bringing in enough new elements to create a refreshing and immersive cinematic experience. Fresh off Adaptation, Nicholas Cage brings another stellar performance, establishing himself as one of the best actors of his generation. It is always a surprise to find a Hollywood film that does not rely on fancy special effects but rather, on provocative character development brought by some of the best actors and actresses in the business. If you're sick and tired of the Fast and Furious franchise and superhero movies, do yourself a favor, go see Matchstick men.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Who's the Real Con Here?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Only the most naive viewer will be surprised by the twist most will see coming a mile away. Like most filmmakers, Ridley Scott assumes his audience is clueless, as epitomized by the cheap film-school tricks he uses to convey Roy's disorder (like the cheap film-school tricks Spielberg used to convey Tom Hanks's "issues" in "Saving Private Ryan.") He rips off not only "The Sting" and "Paper Moon," but "Diabolique" and "The Kid," then tries to convince us that what ultimately happens to Roy is a good thing, with a resolution so predictable and perfectly timed that it's, in itself, a con.Scott, who seems uncomfortable with projects that don't call for tons of CGI, over-edits, but, at least, unlike "Gladiator," you can follow the action. I think the movie would've had more bite were it set in the past, where people were much more trusting, instead of our cynically-drenched times. What redeems "Matchstick Men" is Nicolas Cage as a variation of his goofball self.I will say that, whomever came up with "For some people, money is a foreign film without subtitles" earned his paycheck -- it's the best line of the year.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Appealing characters, a good story, and a life lesson as well give Matchstick Men a 9 out of 10!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Appealing characters, a good story, and a life lesson as well give Matchstick Men a 9 out of 10! In fact, the more one thinks about the plot the more one realizes how the little things work together to make the whole. Unfortunately, it not possible to be more specific without revealing the ending but obviously, those involved in bringing this story to the screen had a solid road map; they knew where they were going! Those who seek to nitpick the plot elements have missed both the fun of the movie and the little things that make the plot work with only a bit of reality suspension. But this comedic drama's (I've heard it called a dark comedy but that really doesn't fit) strength is not the plot, fun and twisting though it may be. The appealing characters that populate the film are what make this movie a fun date!Matchstick Men(2003)is directed by Ridley Scott and stars Nicolas Cage as Roy, a conman suffering from an obsessive compulsive disorder, Sam Rockwell as Frank, Roy's young partner who tries to keep him in working condition and Alison Lohman as his long lost 14-year-old daughter Angela. Cage is great in a part tailored to his style but still not easy to pull off. His character brings to mind the song "Both Sides Now" where "something's lost but something's gained in living every day."Alison Lohman, age twenty-three when the movie was shot, is completely believable as a fourteen old who fights with Mom and runs off to spend time with her newly discovered Dad. She proves she can handle big screen emotions moving us through several mood changes in short order.The supporting cast from partner Frank (Rockwell) to Doctor Klein (Bruce Altman) to the laundry lady (Beth Grant) all do excellent work developing their screen personas in a matter of minutes, personas believable even at the end after the all the twists and turns. Of course the novelist (Eric Garcia), screen writers, and director Scott, all deserve their piece of the credits.Personally, I see about one half the number of movies that I used to see in the theater - can't afford the popcorn anymore! And if I choose poorly, I really feel ripped off. But this one was a good choice - even without popcorn. A word to the wise: see it with someone you love - so you can compare notes later.</fullReview>
	</review>

The characters in the last 10 minutes of the movie are not the same characters that were in the first hour and 50 minutes. 		<shortDescription>If you come with a Matchstick mentality..You get burned!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Match Stick Men- Rated PG-13 for thematic elements, violence, some sexual content and languageNever go to a movie where the lead players are all con men, because you will never look at another movie without second-guessing if the characters are true to themselves or each other. I hate movies like this. It only worked well. And that was in 1973. George Roy Hill's 1973 Oscar winner, The Sting with Robert Redford and Paul Newman. "Matchstick Men" is hoping to build on the confidence that there is honor among thieves. There's the mark and there's the sting and never the two shall meet. Nicolas Cage and Sam Rockwell play con men that take old people and insecure people and the Newvo-rich out to the laundry without them even knowing it. In fact, they give them the money. In lies, Cage as the more successful con artist who has been storing away the money, is a obsessive -compulsive neurotic and has an obsession with dogs. Rockwell is his protégé, but still needs to make the con just to make car payments. After one of his neurotic attacks and loss of `Medication' that goes down the drain literally, Cage goes to a doctor, who talks to him about his past, in which he said, there is an ex-wife who was going to have a baby. Cage gets the number for the ex and has the doc call her to see if she had the baby, If he's a dad, and what does he or she looks like. Then we meet Angela, the daughter and the obsessive-compulsive con artist forms a bond with his newfound daughter. He even takes her on cons and shows her the ropes, because that is what she wants to do with her father. With all this adding up, you throw in the big con that Rockwell is planning. A white washing of foreign money for American rate at a nice profit for the mark and the cons. It's at this point I start to have my doubts. Who is really in on the big con and who's conning whom? This movie reminds me a little of David Mamet's House of Cards. A thriller about a psychiatrist who's one of her patients is a confidence man. She wants to know what's involved and in the end, the con is on her. Basically, she learns that when you play with matches or matchstick men (another name for a con artist) you get burned. In the best movies, the hero or anti-hero in this case finds that the truth shall set you free.and it does. There is an ending that was not as emotionally impacting as I would like but it does work. In the end, you are the Nick Cage character and what happens to him, happens to you. And his happiness is your happiness. And his loss is your loss. And his life is your life. He tries to justify in the movie that his marks are all deserving and that he never hits on any victims that don't have it coming and in the end, he learns that that is not true. I still liked the Sting and I love movies about cons that work. Matchstick Men worked, not because that the con was the plot. It worked because the plot was the con. If Nick Cage gets a nomination for his performance here, it is well deserved and that's no con. - Mr. Cinema</fullReview>
	</review>

Then when she opens up the dog statue and sees the money and the gun, she has a surprised and excited look on her face. Apparently she is thinking something like "Wow, what is my father up to?" I think she actually says out loud to herself "No way!." Again, seems like typical behavior for a teenage girl who just met her father. She even hugs the dog. Is that what con artists do when they are alone?At first I thought she might have been an undercover cop, but what she did when alone in the house made me think that she really was his daughter. Maybe they should have not had any scenes where she is alone in the house. But not having any might get us wondering why not.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-09-13</date>
		<username>Bryan (jdeblasio681@earthlink.net)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A film worthy of appraise!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Overall this film is a visually and mentally challenging picture. From the start of the film i was intrigued and interested in the characters. Nicolas Cage's performance is on the mark and equally powerful as his similar "obsessive compulsive disorder" role in Adaptation. The film progresses strong and the character development is solid which pays off in the end. The climax of the film's story is extremely suspenseful, but director Ridley Scott doesn't leave the audience there. Until the final moments of the film Scott entrances the audience in awe and surprise and ends with a great twist. The only dislike i had with this film was certain camera and editing choices Ridley Scott used in conveying the seriousness of Nicolas Cage character's "obsessive compulsive disorder." In the beginning of the film there were numerous scenes where the editing was tight, quick, and flashy. This was very effective in taking the audience inside the disturbed mind of Nicolas Cage's character. Yet towards the end it became apparent that Scott and the editors just gave up this initial theme and editing pace they had started in the beginning. I wish they had decided to stick with this theme which i felt really expressed Nicolas Cage characters' disturbed mind visually. Also, throughout the film there were cheap - looking transitions from scene to scene which just annoyed me. All in all, this is by far one of the best films I've seen lately in theaters. It is an extremely entertaining, enjoyable, and exciting piece of cinema. I give it a 9 out of a 10. GO SEE THIS FILM.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-09-12</date>
		<username>Dan</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Solid and Consistent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For anyone who has seen [i]Adaptation[/i] you have witnessed what Nicolas Cage can do. You've seen his talent. In [i]Matchstick Men[/i] he steps it up a notch. His character is Roy, an agoraphobic (afraid of the outdoors) with nervous twitches and an obsessive compulsive disorder revolving around threes but only specified to certain actions. However, as long as hes on his meds the symptoms are managable. Cage is incredible in the details he uses to bring out this character. He keeps the twitches consistent and is constantly surprising you from one scene to the next. When paired with the talented Rockwell he excels even more playing off the snappy chemistry between the both of them.Roy and his partner Frank (Sam Rockwell) are polar opposites. Roy likes everything neat and has a closet just for cleaning supplies. Frank lives in a pig-sty of an apartment reminscent of Joes hole in Joes Apartment. But together they make an incredible team who can design and execute some of the most brilliant cons around. But in there next con an obstacle has presented itself. Roy's 14-year-old daughter suddenly arrives on the scene. Interestingly enough she expresses an interest in her Dad's work and quickly becomes involved. But her eagerness and inexperience could prove to be destructive as well fueled by their own twisted motives.Its a brilliant dark comedy. It slides from one genre into the next with the sleight of a magician's hand. Cage delivers more than a few quotable lines and the con games run like capers. But also there are touching father-daughter scenes that reach their climax during a nail-biting car chase through an airport parking garage.The film's atmosphere brought to mind [i]Catch Me If You Can[/i]. Ridley used clothing and decorations to evoke a feel for a past decade. Hans Zimmer and his mix of lounge songs solidified this hypothesis.Although this is not an epic Ridley still showed off his talent. He exploited the close-up and used quick editing more and more as Cage's ever changing condition would worsen. In one particular scene Cage is sittting at a table in front of a door and a woman opens the door to let the dog out. Blinding light fills the screen and we seen a close-up of Cages twitching eyes. Everything through his point of view becomes exaggerated and anxious tightening your own chest. Then as he leaves the house the film calms down as Cage calms down. It was a wonderful technique used effectively throughout the film.In conclusion the film is solid. It is successful in all areas leaving few kinks in its armor. The acting is superb and leads the film through it's innovative plot. Drama/Comedys that work powerfully on both levels are hard to pull off. A film that can have you laugh out loud, sweat in suspense, and reflect on the complex relationships has something few other films have. [i]Matchstick Men[/i] is unqiue and strange, consistently surprising you all the way to when you are blindsided by the twizzelered ending.[b]A-[/b]</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>torqtorq</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Genius director Ridley Scott pulls off another excellent job.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Lead actor Nicolas Cage and director Ridley Scott should be working together more often. Cage's portrayal of Roy, an obsessive-compulsive con-artist, is complemented by Scott's directing to give the audience an accurate idea of what's going on inside the character's head. As the movie progresses, it becomes increasingly easy to estimate Roy's stress level via the frequency and severity of Cage's nervous ticks. Roy, obsessed with cleanliness and uncomfortable in outdoors situations, is played in a more humorous manner than one would expect, and combined with colleague Sam Rockwell's character Frank, the movie is funnier than nearly any comedy released this year, while still managing to be dramatic.A bond between Roy and his recently acquired 14-year-old daughter is the main plot of the movie. After being convinced by con partner Frank to visit a psychiatrist, Roy attempts to contact his ex-wife, resulting in the meeting of his daughter, who he becomes emotionally attached to and suddenly he questions his borderline-illegal actions (much to the dismay of his business associate). 24-year old Alison Lohman does an incredible job of conveying the actions and personality traits of a teenage girl whose tomboyish ways are reminiscent of recent Avril Lavigne fame.Matchstick Men will reach practically any audience with its elements of drama, comedy, suspense, mystery, and action. Being released the weekend after the slow Labor Day weekend ticket sales will certainly help its performance as well. Combine that with an ending as intricate as that of The Usual Suspects or Fight Club, and you have a movie that isn't just conning you out of your money.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>You Can't Con A Con Man, Or Can You?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Attention everyone, rising star on the horizon, or should that be shooting star? Alison Lohman is an incredibly gifted 23 year-old actor. Case in point, this film; she convincingly plays the role of Nic Cage's 14 year-old daughter. Since the only other role I recall seeing her in was as Michelle Pfeiffer's teenage daughter in `White Oleander', who knew she was in her 20's. That aside, this is a well written, well acted look at the mental make-up of a con man and his protégé. It's not a cutsey happy-happy joy-joy kind of story, but it is about trust, family values, and the pursuit of happiness (if that comes with pulling the biggest and best scam of your life). Nicolas Cage has been on the down end on the teeter-totter of his career and finally, someone sat on the other end to raise him back up, almost, to his `Leaving Las Vegas' level. The man's practically flawless in his portrayal of Roy, the `flim-flam man' who's so full of tics and obsessive-compulsive behavior, he makes Jack Nicholson's character in `As Good As It Gets' look perfectly normal. Also notable, is Sam Rockwell as Frank. Without Frank (the protégé), you'd really have no story. Frank is Ying to Roy's Yang and Sam pulls off a brilliant turn as the semi-slimy con artist. I can't give you much more to go on, except to say that I was truly impressed with this gem in a sea of special effects summer movies.</fullReview>

		<username>janyeap</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>It's the supplement that's needed to treat deficiency...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There's Cage's miserly (the doggy--bank connection is an uncanny idea), neurotic and over-cautious recluse, with his OCD problem, matching up with Sam Rockwell's devil-may-care, impulsive weasel. You can expect to see a pair of odd men subconsciously acting up to make the audience laugh. This film's about "taking money away from people who are too stupid not to have it taken from them." The wisecracking jokes are hilarious but they're also telltale clues to some of the unexpected events that follow.When a script brings two characters to support the saying, 'unlike poles attract,' one can expect this film to succeed only if its character development is solid. Oh yes, under Ridley Scott's artfully crafted direction and with the great performances of ALL the cast members, this film is a delight to see. Even the actors with the smallest roles get their moments to pump up their on-screen expressions and become part of the whole film.The dialogue in this film is incredibly sharp. This is one of those truly smart films where events happen that uncannily reflect back to little hints in the passing dialogue. Definitely a great ploy to help with the development of the characters and their personalities! The film makes a play on words a lot. Many of the words, in the dialogue, gradually become puns when connected to events. Even a simple word like 'supplementary' brings important meanings to several sets of events. I had a lot of fun trying to make a recall of them. The film includes many wonderful plot twists and scams - be it at a time the characters are taking the audience to the grimy diners, or the laundromats, or the airport bars, or the convenience stores.Even the last few minutes of the film work well for me, almost as if flowing out the dialogue context preceding this scene ... beautifully summing up the concept that there are non-monetary assets, that to some people, are worth their weight in gold. Alison Lohman's Angela is delightful to watch - the sort of energetic and charismatic young lass that would brighten up a dull home. This film has a terrific choice of soundtracks. A Solid B+</fullReview>

The story is from a novel by Eric Garcia, who writes satirical science fiction, so that is odd in itself, and then the screenplay is by first-time scribe Nicholas Griffin. It was very bold to take a newcomer and a weirdo sci fi source and base a big movie on them. But it was a gamble that paid off in spades. The magic ingredient to this film is Alison Lohman. She had previously devastated everybody emotionally in 'Sharing the Secret', and now she plays an even younger part of a 14 year-old girl. Being very small at five feet two (Shhh! she hates that) and Nick Cage being tall, the size thing works. She is just the cutest, most engaging, smiley, jumping about with glee like a kid teenybopper imaginable. You just want to put her in your pocket and take her home. But there are deep poignant elements to all of this, and I cannot reveal all the reasons, as we get perilously close to spoiler territory if I do. Sam Rockwell, a kind of poor man's Brad Pitt, is wild, wild, wild, just right in fact. This is all so carefully judged. Cage plays a man with nervous tics, phobic and manic-compulsive symptoms, and a tendency to extreme hysteria and panic attacks. He does this to perfection and it does not appear mannered. So hats off! Tics on! I can't imagine how or where he managed to learn how to do all that, unless he has a few crazy relatives or spent some time in the bin. But he pulls it off, that is what counts. He really is a major actor, you just can't get away from it, no matter that he has always inspired reluctance. He does not seem the sort of guy who should be an actor. He is one of those quiet types whom you expect to see at the airport, who is about to get into the cabin and reveal himself as a pilot, you know, the ones with three gold stripes on their cuffs and the silent faraway look. And then suddenly he becomes somebody. It's mystery time! Well, then there is the plot. It is really really slick. You can't imagine, and I can't tell you. Curl up with this on the sofa, you couch potatoes. (And yes, Dan Quayle, potato has an 'e' when in the plural.)</fullReview>

		<username>HelloTexas11</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Ridley Scott's tricky caper</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I don't think I'll ever forgive Nicolas Cage for 'City of Angels;' in fact, I don't like most of the movies I've seen him in. He has however starred in one film that is an all-time favorite of mine, 'Matchstick Men.' Its plot and characters are almost impossible to describe in a short space, and the twist ending ranks right up there with 'The Sixth Sense' as one of the best ever. But you know what? Even if it had a conventional, happy ending that was no surprise at all, 'Matchstick Men' would still be one of the most enjoyable movies of the last decade. Cage plays Roy Waller who, when we meet him, is a small-time con artist with a rash of personality and mental disorders, none of them completely disabling but put together make him quite a mess. In fact, one of the amazing things about the character is that he can usually pull himself together long enough to complete a con job. These tend to be phone scams, telling people they've won some valuable prize and then convincing them to hand over a check through official-sounding double-talk. He and his partner Frank (Sam Rockwell) are quite good at it... until one day when Roy loses it and has a major panic attack. Frank arranges for Roy to see a shrink and from there the film goes off in an unexpected direction (though I'm not sure what the 'expected' direction would be.) Roy meets his daughter Angela (Alison Lohman) for the first time, a teenager he didn't even know existed. Given his profession and range of emotional problems, their time getting acquainted does not go smoothly; she's something of a troublemaker to boot and so that complicates matters. But a rapport develops between them and he takes her under his wing eventually, teaching her the tricks of his trade, probably because he doesn't know what else to teach her. Helping Angela seems to help Roy overcome his own problems to a degree, so he and Frank decide to pull a big con on a wealthy target and Angela becomes involved in that as well, in fact playing a key role at a crucial moment. To tell more beyond this point would ruin this intricately plotted story, spoiler warning or no. Suffice to say there are two major surprises that pull the rug out from under the viewing audience but are so well-done, one is left open-mouthed in admiration. Director Ridley Scott once again displays his professional acumen and versatility here, delighting in bringing this oddball script to life. Nothing ever feels forced, and the surprises when they come really blindside us. Alison Lohman is perfect in her role as a fifteen-year-old; one would never guess that that actress was in real life 24 at the time. Sam Rockwell is very funny and convincing as Roy's partner. And yes, Nicolas Cage succeeds in bringing a very complex and strange personality to life. Roy Waller becomes so believable to us that by the end of the film, we feel we know him as a person, not a character in a movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2007-09-30</date>
		<username>ackstasis</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"Let his greed meet his imagination"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'Matchstick Men' is a film about con-men. Accordingly, we're never sure which facts are true and which are merely constructed as part of a ruse. The film plays games with its audience: just like a good con, we are subtly coerced into placing our trust in the narrative, and that moment of revelation ? when we suddenly realise that we have been completely and utterly duped ? leaves us feeling hollow and foolish. Director Ridley Scott has dedicated much of the last decade to epics, and few can match his skill for constructing cinematic spectacle. Next to such films as 'Gladiator (2000),' 'Black Hawk Down (2001)' and 'Kingdom of Heaven (2005)' (or even the underrated 'Hannibal (2001)'), 'Matchstick Men' might seem a comparatively light effort, and yet Scott's storytelling experience was just what was required for such an intricate story. Though the film is, superficially, a film about con-men (and the twisting, deceptive narrative emulates this), at its heart, 'Matchstick Men' is very much a character study, an exploration of the relationship between a morally-irresponsible, psychologically-unstable father and his estranged fourteen-year-old daughter.Roy Waller (Cage) is a con-man; or, as he likes to declare, a "con artist." Together with his protégé and partner, Frank Mercer (Sam Rockwell), Roy makes a living out of relieving people of their finances. He doesn't particularly consider himself a criminal, justifying his actions by stating that "they give their money to me," citing his victims' greed and naivety as their own downfall. Problematically, especially for somebody who must always present the appearance of respectability, Roy is also a serious obsessive-compulsive, always edgy and constantly cleaning his already-spotless, multi-million dollar condo. In the middle of a potentially profitable long-term con (the shifty victim played by Bruce McGill), Roy discovers through his psychiatrist (Bruce Altman) that he is the father of a 14-year-old girl, Angela (Alison Lohman). As Roy tries to juggle all these happenings in his life, everything he depends upon threatens to fall apart, and he ultimately discovers a part of life that really is worth living.Nicolas Cage really is something of an on-off actor. That he's got talent is beyond question, but, sadly, for every 'Adaptation' there is a 'The Wicker Man.' However, he is positively excellent here, playing his trouble-plagued swindler ? nervous tics and all ? with a quirky combination of comedy and pity. Sam Rockwell once again demonstrates that he really is a quality untapped resource in Hollywood, his character's vulgar, carefree demeanour in direct contrast with Cage's obsessive-compulsive. The main talking point of the film is, however, Alison Lohman who ? at an astonishing 24 years of age ? manages to portray a flawless 14-year-old girl (or, more specifically, she plays an adult passing herself off as a 14-year-old girl, which only makes the performance even more impressive!). I already knew this age discrepancy coming into the film, and I tried on numerous occasions to discern a mature young woman beneath the rosy grin and baggy clothes, but I couldn't see a thing. The disguise is, for lack of a better word, flawless.For most of the film's running time, the viewer is lulled into a false sense of security, wholeheartedly believing that 'Matchstick Men' is something of a feel-good romantic comedy. Just at the climax, this entire notion is yanked from beneath us, as the entire plot is revealed to have been Frank's own elaborate long-con, a meticulously-calculated bid to claim his mentor's substantial bank account. Don't listen to those who claim to have guessed it from the beginning: my bet is that most of them already had prior knowledge that a twist was coming, and so spent the entire film scanning ever scene for clues. Even with my incredible intellect (and please forgive the modesty), the possibility of the twist didn't even click in my mind until Roy had emerged from the make-shift hospital bed. Despite the twist, the film never loses sight of its main purpose, which is a character study of the father-daughter relationship between Roy and Angela. When the former discovers that his entire life for months has been one huge con, his major regret is not the loss of his million-dollar fortune, but the fact that the caring relationship he had developed with Angela had been nothing more than a ruse. Nevertheless, despite being fooled and robbed, Roy is all the better for it. Ridley Scott has orchestrated a wonderful con!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Professionally executed con job</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Matchstick Men" is a glossy, highly professionally made con men movie featuring terrific performances. Director Ridley Scott and cinematographer John Mathieson (both were together on the very differently scaled "Gladiator") have crafted a beautiful looking movie. The various sting plots, which as is standard for the genre don't make 100% sense from the premise on, are helped immeasurably by the leads. Nicholas Cage adds to his range of tics from "Adaptation" and Alison Lohman centers the movie as luminously as she did "White Oleander." Their father/daughter relationship is unusual for a caper flick, and keeps it out of the more typical noir atmosphere, and perhaps unnecessarily reinforced with the coda after the twist uncoils. Are the rat pack music selections supposed to intentionally recall "Ocean's 11"? The movie recalls the 19th century source of the term "confidence man," from the first grift that the police identified, that started with the line "Do you have confidence in me?" In these pro's we do.</fullReview>

		<username>Ethan Ranis</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>About as subtle as a hacksaw</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I came into this movie with fairly low expectations. It was reviewed by most as a family sort of flick, con-man meets daughter who turns life upside down, etc. By the combination of seeing the advertising and reading reviews, it was clear that the film was at least partially trying to cross Ocean's Eleven with some lovey-dovey family angle.But no, it was worse.The film starts out fairly strong, with a lot of camera tricks to emphasize Roy's (the main character, played by Nicholas Cage) obsessive-compulsive disorder. Cage can't seem to strike a line as to his treatment of the character's illness. He's either over-doing it or completely forgetting it. One moment he has five tics in a row, the next he seems perfectly fine. The film also quickly discards all the camera motifs, such as the images of Roy's ordered, contained life being shaded blue, and chaos and the world outside being yellow.A little way in, Sam Rockwell's character Frank (who is sorely underused, by the way) gets Roy a new psychiatrist, as his old one has skipped town. The shrink hooks Roy up with his long-lost daughter Angela (Alison Lohman). Angela is either crying, giggling, gaping or being cute throughout the entire film, until perhaps the ending.Meanwhile, the film opens roughly five other excess sub-plots, such as Roy's blossoming relationship with the woman at the checkout line in a local grocery store (they say "Hi" a few times), and a con on a guy who is very greedy and very rich. The writers box themselves in, strangled by the tangled plot lines.So what do they do? They get ludicrous. Suffice it to say that the big con goes wrong, there's a ridiculous car chase, and the guy who was conned tracks Angela down. Things get more contrived from there, piling on the "surprises" until everyone in the audience either oohs, ahhs, or throws up.To top it all off, there's a completely unnecessary "One Year Later" coda, which returns to the same sentimental mush the film had at its core. The process of groping for a twist ending that would make people think the film was clever has now elongated the film by about 45 minutes.To add to this mess, the film never executes any of its development with subtlety. If it had some finesse, this movie could probably have been decent. Instead, I felt more conned than anyone else on screen.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>phucitol</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A wonderfully entertaining low-impact comedy/crime flick</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Nicolas Cage is one of my top ten favorite actors but this doesn't make me biased towards every film he's appeared in. Some I've liked, others not so much. In Matchstick Men he is cast in one of his better roles much along the lines of "Lord of War" and "Bringing Out the Dead". The type of character that's quirky, misunderstood, completely believable, and at times morbidly funny. You gain a feeling of empathy for him as Roy Waller, an unscrupulous con-man with some of the most unusual mental imbalances, "ticks" and OCD habits. These habits become the focal point of the subtle humor throughout the movie. Sam Rockwell, playing Cage's right hand man in crime as Frank Mercer is an equally quirky and shady character. Lacking the obvious psychological idiosyncrasies as his partner, he spends half his time setting up the "con" job and the other worrying whether Roy has taken his medication and is stable enough to follow through. The chemistry between both as actors and characters nears brilliance.Alison Lohman who plays Angela, Roy's newly introduced (and previously unknown to him) daughter is instantly likable and first appears to be the antithesis of him. Her emotional outbursts are well acted and you will immediately feel sorry for her as much as Roy does. But keep in mind once you've reached this point of familiarity with the characters the viewer is being taken in by the "con" as much as their film victims. All is not what it appears. The story and script is witty and it holds your attention from beginning to end. The cinematography, direction and editing is meticulous and sets the stage perfectly for every scene - down to Roy's bleak, antiseptic lifestyle and brief glimpses of what's really going on in his head. This isn't an action movie per-say. You won't find guns blazing, things blowing up and hateful villains such as in "Con Air" or "Face/Off". This is an intelligent comedy, not snobby or artsy, with a great story and acting throughout. If I was to embellish any further details it would ruin the film for you. Just remember when you think you've figured out what exactly is going on, this does a 180 on you with one of the better endings I've seen in years.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Matchstick Men is a film about con-men, namely Nicolas Cage and Sam Rockwell. Everyone entering his house has to take their shoes off and he wipes things tht other people touch, etc. Odd then, that he appears to have no problems with cigarette smoke staining his ceiling and furnishings, or ash falling onto the carpets. Whilst I can see the use as a means to portray Cage's characters' stress level, I find it counter-productive, and frankly non-plussing, to see it used by an obsessively clean person.That gripe aside, the movie rolls along steadily, if not a little pedestrianly at times. The finale is well managed, though, and of the "everything turns out for the best in the end" school of storytelling.It's worth a look if for nothing else than to see the look on Cage's face when he finds out what tablets he's REALLY been prescribed by his new shrink.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Good performances and slick direction</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Some viewers may think that this is just another con artist film but I think if you take a good look at the performances and the direction by Ridley Scott you might see that this film has something extra to it. Story is about a con man named Roy Waller (Nicolas Cage) who is obsessive compulsive and chain smokes and possesses many tics. His partner and protege is Frank Mercer (Sam Rockwell) and together they Scam people out of money with a prize scheme. They have a rich businessman named Chuck Frechette (Bruce McGill) spotted for a con with a big payoff at the end but one day Roy spills his medication down the drain and his doctor is nowhere to be found. Frank tells him of a doctor to go see so Roy pays him a visit. He meets Dr. Klein (Bruce Altman) and Roy starts confessing what some of his problems are. He mentions that he has a child that he has never seen and asks Dr. Klein to make a phone call to his ex-wife for him. Dr. Klein does and tells him he has a daughter that wants to meet him. Roy meets Angela (Alison Lohman) and she seems to be interested in her father from the start. She comes over to his home and stays for a few days and they become close. Roy tells her the truth about what he does and he even involves her in a scam or two. Roy and Frank bilk Chuck out of a lot of money but Chuck gets a look at Angela and he tracks her down and he has a confrontation at Roy's home. Chuck demands his money back and he also says that he will want more! Ridley Scott shows us again that he can handle more than action films and he reminds us of the director that gave everyone "Thelma and Louise". Scott's direction is slick and the cinematography is very impressive. Their are several shots with rays of sunlight shining through the windows and this gives the film a sleek and slick look. The music score is very interesting also and it seems to be a mixture of 1950's jazz with a European flavor. In some scenes the music sounds like it came right out of a Fellini film. Cage revels in giving us flawed characters and he seems to be well cast here. He does an excellent job of giving different looks to Roy and we actually see his character change during the course of the film. I said last year that Alison Lohman deserved an Oscar nomination for "White Oleander" and her performance here is very impressive. She's actually 24 years old but she plays a girl of 14 and it's totally believable. She's a young actress of extraordinary talent and following her career will be exciting. I thought the twist at the end of the film wasn't necessarily to fool the audience but to be used as a device for Cage to become the good person that he has realized about himself. He is shocked when he discovers what has happened but in essence it was the best thing that ever happened to him. On the surface this material may appear to be familiar but if you look into the performances and the direction you should see that this film does have something extra to offer.</fullReview>

		<date>2005-11-03</date>
		<username>kylopod (kylopod@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Good premise, weak execution</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I expected to like this film. I enjoy movies about con artists, and it looked to me like a return to the quirky types of roles Nicolas Cage used to do back in the '80s. Ever since his rise to stardom, he's frequently been cast in roles where--to me, at least--he has seemed oddly out of place. This is probably because he has an air of unconventionality to him, which often clashes with the tone of what he's in.So when I heard that he was playing an agoraphobic, obsessive-compulsive con man who discovers he has a teenage daughter, I was sure this film would be my cup of tea. It was getting good reviews, and it sounded like an interesting premise, one that cuts across genres. That, unfortunately, turns out to be the main problem. The film tries to do too many different things, and they cancel each other out.We can start with Cage's character. He's shown as a socially inept individual with lots of tics and stammers, a man so fearful of the outside world that he has trouble leaving his own house. The notion that a guy like this could also be a seasoned con artist doesn't ring true. When we see him working, he seems marginally competent at best, and his scams aren't particularly clever or inspired. Maybe I'm spoiled after seeing "Catch Me If You Can," a movie that shows what true brilliance in this profession can look like. But even con artists of lesser skill usually know how to adapt to new situations, a trait this character does not appear to possess. On the contrary, the first scam we see him pull off, he nearly blows. How did this guy stay in the business for twenty years, with as much success as he's said to have had?Not only is it hard for me to believe that he'd be capable of working in such a venue, he doesn't even seem the type who would want to. He has too much of a conscience, as becomes evident when he develops a relationship with his newfound teenage daughter, played by Alison Lohman. I realize the point is supposed to be that the Lohman character is bringing out feelings in him that he didn't know he had, but from the beginning he seems too moral for his own good. If he's irresponsible, it's not because he doesn't care, but because he's overly absorbed in his own problems. Some of his scenes with her are entertaining, but ultimately they don't go anywhere satisfying. All the film achieves in the end is plot manipulation.Perhaps Cage was not the right choice for this role after all. I can think of several actors who might have done a better job. One that springs to mind is Bill Murray. He played a severely phobic character in "What About Bob?," and while his performance in that film was over-the-top, he has shown from his other work that he can reign in his comedic talent when he needs to. It may not have saved the entire film, but it would at least have provided a more convincing starting point.</fullReview>

		<date>2011-09-21</date>
		<username>tieman64</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Blade Runners</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Implants! Those aren't your memories. They're somebody else's. They're Tyrell's niece's." - Deckard Ridley Scott's answer to Spielberg's "Catch Me If You Can", "Matchstick Men" is a slick but lightweight comedy thriller about a con artist, played by Nicholas Cage, who suffers from OCD and agoraphobia. The film, which mixes scenes of family bonding with cool con artistry, eventually reveals itself to be a con on both audience and Cage's character.Though David Mamet and Neil LaBute routinely cover such material in a superior manner, "Matchstick Men" is nevertheless one of Scott's better films. And like Scott's only great film, "Blade Runner", it is very much a film about neuroses and surfaces (Cage spends the film both obsessed with objects and unaware that they are in fact manipulating him).Scott's filmography is one of pretty pictures, one which puts primary emphasis on surfaces, looks, aesthetics, the external, outer facades, light and decorative arrangements. In contrast, "Blade Runner" is about a dour character, Rick Deckard, who confronts the fact that all men are machines programmed by impersonal, outside forces; all identity is a matter of engineering. For Deckard, what constitutes essence, depth, authenticity and Self suddenly then becomes a fiction. There is no subject, only matter. All identity is a mechanistic construction, nothing is given, all is artificially produced, the "subject" is borne of an ornamental void, and clinging to pretty pictures, photographs, designer visuals and memories no longer help but perpetually torment. For Deckard, the outside then becomes nothing, mere code to be chewed, aesthetics become vapid and all environmental objects point to Antonioni-like depthlessness. Consciousnes is itself called into question. Consciousness, like memory and habit, is always a reflection on, or after effect of, the unconscious processes which produce it (some neuroscientists themselves believe that actions, thinking, learning and intention all precede consciousness, are preconscious, thereby negating free-will), and the always too-late attempt by a subject to grasp the "moment" will only ever produce neurosis. "Blade Runner" doesn't subvert Scott's aesthetic, but captures a kind of painful, melancholic, hollowness behind it all. In contrast, all of Scott's other films pass off kitschy style as depth, and pretty postcards as "importance", "content", "authenticity" "meaning" and "auteurism".So perhaps this is why "Matchstick Men" remains one of Scott's more tolerable films. In being about misdirection, programming, engineering and the falsity of images, it, like "Blade Runner", negates some of Scott's more grating tendencies as an artist. Note too that it is the promise of sex, family and companionship that allows Cage's reality to be re-engineered, a common P.K. Dick theme, in which the author's future men frequently find themselves engineering babies, pets and animals to engender feelings of "purpose" and assuage an innate human Lack; "I know it's not real, but it's real to me, completes me, makes me whole, and that's all that matters." The film then ends with Cage substituting his simulational "fake family" for a supposedly "real one", pregnant wife and all.7.9/10 ? Worth one viewing. See the con movies of Neil LaBute and David Mamet.</fullReview>
	</review>
