		<username>oneguyrambling</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>An efficient and fairly entertaining misdirection flick.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Matchstick Men arrived and vanished without a trace because the film has been made many times before - not necessarily better made but definitely made. And with a film like this the ceiling is quite low, meaning even if it is well executed it can still only ever be fairly entertaining.So perhaps it is high praise when I tell you this is efficient and fairly entertaining? Roy (Nicolas Cage) and Frank (Sam Rockwell) are small time conmen, keeping things low key enough to avoid serious investigation whilst still enabling their nefarious lifestyle. They diddle little old ladies and the too trusting alike, but never to any massive extent. They use various ploys and various aliases to get what they want.Roy's life is ruled by repetition and superstition, he is fully OCD and everything must be done in an order and way that is logical, at least to him. If this daily routine is altered in any way by Roy or anyone else he loses the plot. One example has Roy clean his already clean house from top to bottom. Pills keep Roy (mostly) on the straight and narrow and aware of his restrictions. Funnily enough for someone with so many cleanliness issues Roy chain-smokes throughout the entire film.This all changes when one day Roy loses his pills and finds that his regular Doc has retired. Roy also finds that his ex-wife has previously never told him that they may have a child, this is all a bit much for Roy. Upon consulting a new Doctor Roy asks the Doc to call his ex to make sure, and he is amazed to learn of the existence of Angela, now 14 years old.At the doctor's insistence Roy meets with Angela, he finds that she is a spunky and confident tomboy, quick witted and very smart, suddenly all seems to be going well in Roy's life and he tells Frank that it is time to go for the "big score".After a time Angela moves into Roy's home, being a teenager this creates significant issues with Roy's condition and when one evening he comes home to find the house in disarray Roy gets more than a little wound up. After an argument and a temporary daddy-daughter estrangement Roy decides to open up to Angela as to exactly what he does for a living, instead of revulsion Angela expresses a desire to learn the ropes, leading to some basic training in "scamming for dummies". When this goes wrong Roy decides at the last minute to ditch his normal order and bring Angela along.This initially goes well, then very badly.After the botched scam Roy starts to realise the risks that his career poses for his new relationship with Angela, Roy decides that perhaps exiting the game for good might be the appropriate course of action. After all he has already amassed a considerable amount of cash through many years of low level scamming. Frank tries to talk Roy out of changing his mind or simply targeting bigger ticket marks, but sh*t gets complicated first.The movie ends shortly after Roy realises the dangers of the illegal game that he was a part of for so long. The final acts seem pretty nifty at the time but don't hold up very well to close scrutiny, especially if you consider the chain of events that need to happen to enable them to happen.It's basically hard to explain without giving things away but can I just say that there is a sequence of events that is implausible to say the least.Final Rating - 7 / 10. The movie is entertaining and for a long while captures your attention, just try not to think too hard, you'll end up second guessing a lot of what happens.</fullReview>

		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Plot predictability and the con genre. (spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Of the films, both comedy and drama, that I have seen in the con men genre, this has to be one of my least favorite for the sheer reason that 1) the main character is given far too much attention is and his incredibly anal personality makes him a rather boring spectacle, and 2) it provides too many clues to the audience, which lead to a rather predictable plot. Although, the latter may just be a result of my having seen too much of the con men genre and thus, have some idea of which details are essential and what kind of character interaction I should expect. Even where such details create an obvious storyline, some films like Following and Criminal have done it well enough to at least satisfy, if not altogether wow it's audiences. Matchstick Men doesn't seem to me to have that same effect. It was a great idea, what unfolds in this story of two con men and the reunion of one with his estranged daughter, but something there just lacked the pizazz and I have yet to figure out what it is.Nicholas Cage plays an obsessive compulsive con man. An unusual character combination. He and his partner/protégé, Frank Mercer (Sam Rockwell who, like another viewer suggested was indeed not given nearly enough screen time as he should have), are involved in a con job involving a rather large sum of money. It seems like an easy job and Roy and Frank are just waiting for the payoff.In the meantime, Roy (Cage) becomes debilitated when he runs out of medication needed to control his compulsive behavior. When he tries to contact this therapist and learns that the guy left town, he hires a new one who is pivotal to reuniting Cage with his estranged daughter who is purported to be living with his ex-wife. And this is where Ridley Scott's own con begins.The rules of the con men genre are simple. Those who seem like the most inexperienced cons, or at least secondary grifters, are usually just as observant and capable as their mentors (see Criminal). There is always a con being played, even at times when the audience may've been told that it has not yet begun or was completed at some point (see Following). And every character, no matter how innocent, is usually involved in one way or another. That is usually why the casts are so small, typically involving two or three main characters and three or four supporting characters. No on in innocent and the twists of the story require using these characters to out-con the cons, especially the primary cons. And Matchstick Men plays by every rule.</fullReview>
	</review>

Their main target: old and lonely people who are easy to manipulate and eager to believe what they are told. Each time they sell one of their 'exclusive', 'outstanding', 'incredible' water filtration systems for 5 to 10 times the actual price, they earn a few hundreds of dollars. Not exactly much, but all together it makes a nice living. But Frank's private life isn't as successful as his business. He's an obsessive-compulsive agoraphobic, who needs a clear routine and a psychoanalyst just to keep him in working order. He doesn't even have time for personal relationships, until he's confronted with the fact that he has a 14-year old daughter who would love to meet him. At first his whole world is turned upside-down, but gradually he develops paternal feelings for the teenage girl that wants nothing more but to follow her dad in his professional footsteps...This movie really has several good things to offer and was a nice surprise. Next to the excellent performances of Nicholas Cage and Sam Rockwell, I was surprised by Alison Lohman's performance. She did an excellent job as the teenage daughter. But not only the acting was more than average, so was the story. It was funny and touching at the same time and really offered everything that I look for in a movie. As I already said earlier in my review: it's one of Cage's best movies. I give it a 7.5/10 at least, although an 8/10 isn't too much for this excellent movie either.</fullReview>

Finally Frank comes over to see him because he isn't returning or answering calls. Frank gets him to go see a shrink.The shrink, Dr. Klein, puts Roy on a "new medication," which Roy does not realize until later are just placebos. It helps Roy a little bit, but not much. Then Roy decides that he's going to call his ex-wife to see what happened to the baby that she was carrying in her womb when he left her. He can't bring himself to talk to her, so he gets Dr. Klein to give her a call. Then Klein gives Roy a call and lets him know that his fourteen year old daughter, Angela (Lohman) would really like to meet him.The movie has two primary focuses. The first is the relationship between Roy and Angela. It's several twists and turns, and if you're pretty smart, you might be able to figure out some of them before the credits roll, but I didn't.The movie probably won't end how you expect it to, but that's part of what makes it so great.Bottom Line: 4 out of 4 (own this movie)</fullReview>

(spoilers) About midway through the film, I was simply waiting for it to end. I was completely convinced that Cage was going to do one last score, his daughter would get taken hostage or something, he would have to give up his biggest payoff ever to save the life of this little girl that he just met, and he would suddenly find it within himself to give up his life of crime and start being a legitimate and loving father. There were spectacular performances in this film, the comic relief was abundant and effective, and the film never once panders to exactly what the audience wants to see. So here are the problems that I can see people having with this film. It might be seen as one of those movies that deliberately leads the audience in the wrong direction and then pops this twist on them at the end, and while that may not be completely untrue, it doesn't do it in a way that leaves you feeling cheated. Every movie with a successful twist has to lead you in the wrong direction to a certain extent, but it is whether or not it was obvious or at least cleverly crafted that makes the difference, and Matchstick Men is VERY cleverly crafted.Perhaps more pressing than the concern of leading the audience in the wrong direction, Matchstick Men does, in fact, have a problem with being a little bit too convenient. It is a very complex story, which makes it extremely entertaining, but this also gives it the feeling (especially when the girl happens to walk into her father's work at the carpet store at the end of the film) that everything just worked out too perfectly. Sure, deciding to buy carpet with her loser boyfriend at just the wrong carpet store is perfectly plausible, it may seem strange that she did it exactly six months after ripping him off. But of course, as always, Nicholas Cage delivers a great performance as the slightly disturbed but highly amusing Roy Waller, and Sam Rockwell draws on some of his best performances, especially his performance as Billy the Kid in The Green Mile, and takes the best parts while still adding new dimensions to his iconography. Outstanding.</fullReview>

		<username>laursene</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Il Bidone, reworked</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I know Matchstick Men was based on a novel, but Ridley Scott's film is actually a very amusing reworking of Il Bidone, Fellini's classic (yet regrettably little-seen) mid-50s opus about three con-men in postwar Italy. Their neuroses and distinct personality traits form the core of the tension in both films, and although Il Bidone has a much darker ending, the emotional payload is the same: the aloneness of these men. Scott's meticulous attention to the details of performance is almost on a par with Fellini's (which is saying something, especially with the 50s films), and Cage and Rockwell do a fine job making their characters funny and poignant.Other comparisons: Whereas in Il Bidone, the daughter of the Broderick Crawford character was only in one scene and was more of an emotional catalyst than an active player in the story, here the "daughter" obviously has a larger role, and mostly for the better. Also, parts of Hans Zimmer's score slyly suggest elements of Nino Rota's music for Il Bidone. Overall verdict: Not another irrelevant remake, but a most imaginative reworking.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2004-06-23</date>
		<username>rzajac</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Homage to Fellini</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is "La Dolce Vita" "lite" for those of us who may have trouble parsing the densely packed Fellini original for the moral. I was thinking about the line from one of Paul's epistles where he says, "There is a way that seems right to a man, but the end is death." Fellini's "Vita"--and now Matchstick Men ("MM")--are entrants in the canon of films that explore this idea. It takes more work to get this out of "Vita," because the hero (Marcello Rubini, played by Mastroianni) is so clean-cut and well-intentioned--we could all plausibly want what he wants--so Fellini had his work cut out for him driving us to the conclusion that, no, we *don't* want what Marcello wants; the end thereof is death. The anti-heroes from MM are pre-fabed lost souls; we are quite unlikely to want what they want, and are chomping at the bit to see something give. So when Cage's daughter shows up, we can almost hear the strains of Bobby Darin singing "The Good Life" returning to us in our mind's ear.Add to all this the sweet, almost reverential, use of Rota's "Vita" incidental music in the "airport" and there's no mistaking it; Ridley Scott most assuredly had some tipping of the chapeau to Fellini in mind as he worked out the details of this movie. Fellini fans should take note and indulge in a controlled swoon during this sequence.So: Is the film good? Here's my experience. Like many others commenting in these pages, I felt left a little high and dry by the ending--it just wasn't realistic, and I felt the mythic payload of the movie didn't compensate; it didn't feel like the suspension of disbelief required to accept the ending paid off adequately. However, the film grew on me over the next day. I went back and watched it again, and started to warm up to the overarching theme; the trap of the "sweet life." MM is an accessible "Vita." Some people were put off by the film's final shot before they fade to black. I wasn't. Call me a sappy romantic greenass, but I was deeply touched. This *very* lost soul has finally figured it out; and he got his lesson from life experiences that had been architected to con him. Fascinating!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Who's really being conned here?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is much to like in this quirky tale of two con-men, Cage and Rockwell, who set themselves up for the "big sting" they can finally retire on. Cage is ultimately engaging as the obssessive compulsive Roy Waller who discovers at the most inopportune moment that he is the proud owner of a free-spirited fourteen year old daughter. Unfortunately however several other factors conspire to detract from the film's overall impact.While Cage adds effortlessly to his gallery of 'troubled' screen characters - the nervous tics especially he affects well, the film's climactic 'twist' has been telegraphed for the best part of an hour and fifteen minutes. Furthermore, saturation pre-publicity that actress Alison Lohman who plays Angela, Waller's 14 year old daughter Angela, is in fact 24, totally beggars the question "now WHY would they need an actress of that age to play a young schoolgirl? The more you dwell on that point, the quicker the film is totally ruined for anyone of even the vaguest intelligence.It has been suggested that MATCHSTICK MEN contains no Ridleyesque signature artwork. In fact, his trademark blue filtered light is in evidence in many scenes if you care to look a little harder. As I said at the outset, the film is far from being a loser, Cage's performance alone nails that tight...but from the half-way point onwards it degenerates significantly. The trite ending simply does not do the film justice at all.Ultimately, and it pains me to say this, MATCHSTICK MEN is way less than either its early promise or the sum of its disordered parts.</fullReview>

		<date>2003-09-28</date>
		<username>Chris Knipp</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The old con within a con</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>[W A R N I N G : S P O I L E R S!]Ridley Scott's Matchstick Men is from a novel by semi-cult writer Eric Garcia. The premise is well publicized: a con man who suffers from various forms of compulsive behavior discovers that he has a fourteen-year-old daughter, and for a while - spanned by the movie - she becomes a part of his life. Meanwhile his partner pushes him into the big score he's long avoided.Matchstick Men is the opportunity for the hardworking Nicolas Cage to take on another challenge: playing a loveable neurotic who's also a serious criminal. A con job, a father-daughter story, and a psychological portrait all in one, this screenplay takes on a bit more than it can handle. Even though Ridley Scott does his best to give it deftness and rhythm, the movie doesn't fully develop as either crime drama or family drama. It doesn't show character in depth like Stephen Frears' The Grifters; doesn't study con games and con artists in detail like Nine Queens; and isn't as exciting and suspenseful as crime thrillers like The Italian Job or Rififi. The fun, and fun there undeniably is, is in watching the actors and filmmakers try to distract us from these shortcomings. At moments they do succeed. But then you realize you've been conned, and you haven't gotten to the bottom of the crime, the con mentality, or the neurotic personalities of the principals.As the movie opens Roy (Cage, the compulsive) and partner-protégé Frank (Sam Rockwell, who's more of an adrenalin junky and wastrel) are involved in `short cons,' What they're doing now is using fake phone prizes to get into people's bank accounts. Roy can function well living alone in his immaculate house, so long as he has his meds. But when they suddenly run out (one wonders why a compulsive would run out of anything: he has two or more of every household cleaner), Roy goes bonkers, and then finds that his usual source of meds has left town. Frank finds Roy a psychiatrist named Klein to help Roy get his meds, and Klein not only gives Roy some new and different pills but also persuades him to come in for continuing therapy. This brings out Roy's need to know if his ex-wife had a kid, and he gets Klein to call the wife for him. Before long fourteen-year-old Angela (Alison Lohman) enters Roy's life.Angela is a feisty, tomboyish girl with rough pigtails, a skateboard, sparkling eyes and cheekbones like Mariel Hemingway, as well as a surprisingly strong urge to learn about the grifter life. Against his better judgment Roy shows Angela some elementary cons and she turns out to have a natural gift for the work. Roy is torn between the belief that he ought to keep Angela far away from such business and the certain knowledge that it is the best way for them to bond, which he ardently desires.Matchstick Men is (like Nine Queens but more simply) a con within a con, which seeks to con the audience. The trouble with this device is that when we go back and analyze the way we were conned, it makes the cons in the story themselves tend to crumble. Little effort is given, in fact, to making the men's climactic `long con,' in which they plan to take a lot of money from a nasty rich man (Bruce BcGill) either convincing or well fleshed out. If the whole movie is a con on us, can we trust its portrait of the grifter life? The one time that real con men are honest is when they're explaining their cons.The man who best knows Roy, the compulsive-neurotic con artist Nick Cage plays, is obviously his partner and protégé, Frank (the wild and crazy Rockwell is fairly well used here), who's as much a wastrel and slob as Roy is tidy and anal, and it's Frank's con - also foisted on us as well as Roy - to invent the fourteen-year-old daughter who cons Roy out of his whole stash of cash. Even if you don't realize that till late in the game, the whole network of cons and characters eventually shatters.This plot device is as weak as most of the cons. As Nine Queens (a fuller portrait of con artists) points out, grifters are on guard against other grifters, and an experienced con artist won't fall for his protégé's con. Matchstick Men gets around this problem by presenting a hero who is sympathetically flawed: Roy's mildly disabling ticks and phobias make us feel for him and then he turns out to be a complete softy. The girl's evaluation is to say, `You're not a bad man. You're just not a good one.' And yet he's an ace at his trade. `You don't seem bad,' the daughter says when she's met him. `That's why I'm good,' he answers.But is that believable? Is that enough? There's something missing from the portrait of Roy. All that Cage gets across to us are the tics and the lovability. He does a fine job of that, but he doesn't round out his portrait with any sense of the edge of cruel brilliance that would explain how Roy could have accumulated a huge stash of dough in a safety deposit box without ever being arrested.It's exciting and fun when Roy and Frank, Roy driving with his daughter, head for the airport to complete the big `long con.' Both male actors contribute to this, but Lohman is at her least sympathetic during this sequence.Frears' The Grifters has a detailed and memorable mother-son relationship (Angelica Huston and John Cusack) as well as a story of hers with her boss, and that way the movie explores the soullessness and cruelty of con artist relations in real, chilly depth. By comparison Matchstick Men's father-daughter relationship is slight; it's not developed through a whole movie like Paper Moon's. In the end it's just a bit of lively playing around. The movie keeps us amused for a while, but it leaves us hungry. There are no real payoffs in Matchstick Men, nor does it ever quite decide what it is.My guess is that the injection of both more comedy and more violence, a la Pulp Fiction, might have made it work better. But I don't know. Maybe the filmmakers just conned themselves into thinking they had something here when they didn't; maybe Eric Garcia did too.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The Switch</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After Ridley Scott made a monstrous comeback with Gladiator, Hannibal, and Blackhawk Down, he chose this topic. For whatever reason, Matchstick Men is superior in most areas than its bigger budget brothers. The acting, Cage in particular, is better and the visual trickery and goofiness of Scott shines better than Spielberg did with Catch Me if You Can. Just when I thought I understood him as a director, he goes and changes gear on us again. First I thought he'd be stuck in a fantasy genre after Legend, then an obscure drama line of work with G.I. Jane. Now he's gone from pompous blockbuster supporter to film guerrilla. Obviously he is smarter than James Cameron, Sydney Pollack, George Lucas, Quentin Tarantino, and--perhaps--even Oliver StoneAlas, I cannot really say the same for Alison Lohman. In my book, the two best child-actor performances have always been Christian Bale in Empire of the Sun and Natalie Portman in Leon. Granted, those roles weren't perfect, but they utilized the persona and attitude of the child rather than have some young adult try the role. More memories popped up in my head during Matchstick Men of the "kids" in The Breakfast Club rather than anything truly credible. Fortunately, the director has better things in mind...Scott truly is a visual maestro and everything he's done in the last 12 years has only been an exploitation of that visual grandeur, not caring about the relative comfort of script and acting. Perhaps Thelma &amp; Louise can really be considered his last true film where the actors were allowed to let lose with their own nuances, certainly compared to the operatic emptiness of Hannibal and the cacophony of combat in 'Blackhawk'. Sadly, Hans Zimmer's music isn't a help this time around and really ruins a lot of the moods.Overall, a trademark Scott false-ending, a mediocre storyline, and one of Cage's quirkier performances make this good, but not great. I am happy that Nicolas has since abandoned the money roles of The Rock and Con Air for better work. 3 out of 5 stars.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Matchstick Men: 7/10</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>At first, it's hard to believe that Matchstick Men is a Ridley Scott film. He's mainly known for his gruesome films, like Hannibal and Alien. However, for each Black Hawk Down, there's a Thelma and Louise. Matchstick Men is by no means a violent film, nor hard to watch. In fact, out of all of the movies of 2003 so far, I'll bet that this movie has more rewatch value than most. What really makes Matchstick Men a realistic piece is the acting, which I will get into later.Nicolas Cage, in hopefully another Oscar nominated role, plays Roy, a con man (artist, as he would correct you) with obsessive-compulsive disorder. He and his partner Frank (Sam Rockwell) aren't in the big-time, but still collect decent sums of money. However, he runs out of his pills he needs to control some of his OCD, and his doctor's moved, so he goes to a psychiatrist (Bruce Altman) in order to get some more. In the course of it, he decides to see if his wife, who was pregnant when they were divorced, has a child. Lo and behold, she does, and 23-going-on-14 Angela (Alison Lohman) is introduced into his life.Roy, obviously, doesn't know the first thing about parenting, which doesn't work well with Angela's semi-rebellious behavior. Also, to thicken the plot even more, Roy and Frank are about to perform a huge con on someone, and don't want anyone in their way. Roy doesn't want Angela to know about his real profession (he tells everyone he's into antiquing). And then things start to heat up.Going into Matchstick Men, I was expecting something like Ocean's Eleven, a light-hearted crime caper (not necessarily a comedy), and that's what I got. Although it's not as entertaining nor as confusing as Ocean's Eleven, they're both good movies in their own right. Matchstick Men runs too long at about 110 minutes, but I never felt like checking my watch or wanting to leave. I have read a little of the book by Eric Garcia (it didn't really keep my interest), but not much. Therefore, throughout the movie, I had no idea what was going to happen. At the ending `twist', I predicted it right before Roy did, so I was happy, not only for myself for being able to guess it, but how Scott made the movie go out, I had no idea.Cage, unlike Adaptation., deserves whatever awards or nominations he manages to get. Not only did he make me believe that, yes, he was Roy the con man, but he was also Roy the obsessive-compulsive. The only other actor I have seen portray someone with OCD so well is Tony Shalhoub as Adrian Monk on TV's Monk. The way that he acted was so realistic. However (this is a script complaint), about halfway through or so, he basically lost his OCD. Anyway, Rockwell added another impressive performance, but he didn't have much to do/work with. Lohman impressed me, very much so. I didn't expect so much out of her.This may be considered going off on a tangent, but it makes sense that Robert Zemeckis is an executive producer. He's a protégé of Steven Speilberg, possibly most famous for his false endings. If the last ten minutes or so had been slashed off, then maybe Matchstick Men could have gotten a higher rating. However, it wasn't, so the rating is a solid 7/10.My rating: 7/10Rated PG-13 for thematic elements, violence, some sexual content and language.</fullReview>

It seems like he has to somehow keep everything else about himself perfect because of the guilt he carries around because of what he does. I found I was really able to relate to the this idea of having to keep all these secrets and how it can affect your life more than you think. On the subject of Alison Lohman's part: She is by far the most realistic and talented actress I have ever seen. Is she even acting? She made her part so real that It didn't even seem like a movie. She made me believe that I was living the life of this con artist finding a new unfelt and unconditional love of a father for his daughter. I have never been so emotional during a movie. It seemed like she was fixing my life like she was fixing her fathers. She made me want to be a better person in the same way she made her father feel. She made me want to love her like her father wanted to. She also destroyed my life in the same way his is when he finds out it was all a lie. When he sees her again all you want him to do is grab her and say "Don't you know I loved you?" but you can feel a fathers relief when he knows that she is alright and that she is happy and he will see her again. The Production and direction of this film were perfect. Ridley Scott is amazing and grabbed me by the heart and never let go. He made me want to be an honest man. Alison if you ever read this, thank you and keep up the good work. Your acting truly inspires me.</fullReview>

		<username>The_Void</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A very solid thriller from Ridley Scott</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Matchstick Men is Ridley Scott's return to form after a rather torrid decade with the 1990's. While the film is not as great as his masterpieces; classics such as Blade Runner and Alien, Matchstick Men is still a very solid entry into the con artist sub-genre. I'm a big fan of con-artist films, especially ones that are done well and so I really enjoyed this one. The plot follows Roy Waller, a highly neurotic con man with an estranged daughter, Angela. Roy does his cons with his partner Frank, and the two have just hatched a plan to con a rich businessman out of his hard earned cash. The film benefits from great performances, most notably from Alison Lohman as Roy's daughter. Her performance is good in it's own right, but it's even more amazing when you consider that the actress playing the fourteen year old girl is actually twenty four! Nicolas Cage puts in a nice, if slightly over the top performance; but he always ensures that the action stays interesting, and even Sam Rockwell does well with his role; although he's definitely the third party in this movie.Matchstick Men takes obvious influence from a range of sources, but most notably the Peter Bogdanovich masterpiece; Paper Moon. The way that Roy interacts with his daughter is reminiscent of that movie, but it's also different enough to give Matchstick Men a style of it's own. Ridley Scott is on top form with this movie, and his direction is a huge advantage for it. The pace of the film always suits what's happening on screen; it's thrilling when needs to be, mysterious when it needs to be, and it's even quite touching at times. Matchstick Men is one of those films that are easy to view, and it's hugely enjoyable for that reason; but unlike many films in it's class, Matchstick Men stands out because it offers a very potent commentary on the term 'crime doesn't pay'. Also, as is the standard with thrillers like this; I'm happy to report that the twist is superbly handled, and gives the movie a nice payoff. On the whole, I don't hesitate to recommend Matchstick Men to anyone. Though it doesn't set the cinematic world on fire, it offers a good time and is populated with intriguing and interesting characters. Good stuff!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>210west</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Charmless characters, preposterous plot</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've never seen Nicolas Cage turn in a bad performance, but here the role -- an obsessive-compulsive who also has something of a split personality -- offers so many actorly bits of business that the ham in Cage just can't resist; he gives us just too damned many blinks, tics, and twitches (and so does the director, who uses the character's mental state as the excuse for lots of flashy cutting and jerky camera movement). Worse, there seems no connection between the two halves of Cage's character: When he's in his OCD state, he's as exaggerated as a cartoon, and when he's supposedly "medicated" and working a con, he's too icily cool and resourceful to be believed. Still, even when he's overacting in an extremely unrealistic, Hollywoodish part, Cage is likable. I can't say the same for Sam Rockwell, as his partner in crime (it's hard to figure out why anyone would ever want to see this actor again); and least appealing of all is Alison Lohman, introduced to us as Cage's teenage daughter. Lohman, who's actually older than the role she plays, is something rare for a slim young actress: Shrill, stiff, and thoroughly charmless, she manages to make a creepy, unpleasant role even creepier. Because "Matchstick Men" is a three-character movie, with the main character pretty implausible and the two supporting characters fairly repulsive and impossible to care for, watching it becomes tedious pretty quickly. It's gotten some surprisingly sympathetic reviews, but I'm baffled: The audience when I saw it seemed tired and disappointed.</fullReview>

I enjoy con men movies very much, but this is one of the worst and has very serious script problems: 1) The big "twist" is way too predictable for any audience member that's actually paying attention and it's difficult to believe that Nicolas Cage, who is supposed to be very bright and good at what he does, would fall for it; 2) Said big twist is a major miscalculation because it completely invalidates the only part of the movie that was actually working--the blossoming father daughter relationship; 3) The attempt to wring a happy ending from the dark twists of the script's final act feels completely phony--in fact, if Nicolas Cage had turned into a werewolf at the end and eaten the rest of the cast it wouldn't have been any more implausible than what actually makes it to the screen. Matchstick Men is very bad. Stay away. 		<shortDescription>An enjoyable mix of con artist cool and family bonding</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Roy is neurotic and phobic but it doesn't stop him and his partner Frank being successful conmen. After changing therapists and medication, Roy decides to do a big score with Frank to finish them off. As the score starts, Roy's 14-year-old daughter (whom he has never seen) arrives to spend time with him. When she starts to work out who he actually is during the working day he starts to bond with her and teach her the tricks of his trade - tricks that he will need her to perform.When I went to the cinema to see this my friend took too long in the shops and we missed the start time of it and ending up going into Raising Victor Vargas. Because I owe this film the thanks for seeing that film, I felt I should still see Matchstick Men because it still appealed to me as a film. I rented it at the same time as the film Confidence and, having first watched that, I expected Matchstick Men to be more of the same sort of slick and cool con artist capering with all that that entails. However it was a pleasant surprise to find that this is much more than just a collection of slick ingredients.The film starts as a con artist movie, but doesn't really present itself in the same way as other con artist films do - there is no excess of slick music, visuals or dialogue. However it is still pretty cool in it's own right. What it does do well though is to fill out the plot with something other than the usual collection of twists and turns that the rest of the genre will usually use as their sole device for holding the audience in. While the film does have some good twists and turns, the heart of the film is the core that sees Roy bonding with Angela. If anything, this section does almost too well at involving the audience - a problem that created a rather lame ending that smacks of compromise after a test audience viewing. For the majority of the film, the two threads are weaved together very well and it works to make them seem as all one story rather than two separate aspects of the film.Scott plays with out emotions well, ultimately playing a con of sorts on the audience as much as the characters are playing on their marks. The story is slick in several ways, but not just a superficial manner that you'll be used to if you watch other genre thrillers. Likewise, Scott and his casting team have done a great job with the casting of the film. Cage has done too many action blockbusters lately to still be considered a really good actor but he does well here. He is given a difficult role in that he could have almost been a comedy character with his ticks etc, but he delivers them without letting them control the character or become irritating. Rockwell has too little screen time but is very good; he adds lots of little touches that fill out his character well. Lohman would be a great child actress were it not for the fact that she was actually 22 and not 14! She is really good here and she is a big reason why the family threads all work.Overall this is a pretty enjoyable and involving film - albeit not in the way I expected from a film of this genre. Where often the genre suffers from style over substance but here substance is actually more to it than style. The threads all work well together and it engaged me no problem, only a rather pat final scene spoils an enjoyably bleak conclusion.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>MATCHSTICK MEN (2003) **** Nicolas Cage, Sam Rockwell, Alison Lohman, Bruce Altman, Bruce McGill, Sheila Kelley. Excellent adaptation of Eric Garcia's splendid novel about a phobic, walking tic con man (Cage in another of his finely honed, nuanced performances that has Oscar nom written all over it) and his glib partner (Rockwell equally excellent) whose days of scamming as flim-flam artists have nearly reached their nadir when in walks in his long-lost teenage daughter (the amazing Lohman who also should get an Academy nod) to further complicate things. Cage is brilliant as the coming-apart-at-the-seams neurotic mess who finds his life gaining meaning when he inexplicably bonds instantly to his daughter who only wants to be loved and to learn the game of the grift. Sharply, biting and witty screenplay by the brothers Griffin, Ted (who excelled on the 'Ocean's 11' remake, in this the first sibling collaboration) and perfect direction by veteran Ridley Scott underlining the pathos with heart. Funny and full of nice little plot twists. One of the year's best.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Matchstick Men is a film directed by Ridley Scott.It was based on Eric Garcia's novel Matchstick Men: A Novel about Grifters with Issues with the screenplay written by Ted Griffin and Nicholas Griffin.It stars Nicolas Cage, Sam Rockwell, and Alison Lohman.Matchstick Men is about neurotic con man Roy,who suffers from several emotional problems, including obsessive-compulsive disorder. He and his partner Frank swindle people out of money by posing as money collectors who promise things like tax refunds, package vacations, and other fabulous prizes that they never get. Frank wants to pull a really big job, but Roy is too consumed with fear and panic attacks to join him. Only cigarettes and his trusty illegal prescription drugs seem to keep him going. When Roy finds himself in desperate need of more pills, he is forced to see legitimate psychotherapist Dr. Klein. Roy ends up talking about his emotional damage from a troubled marriage and divorce, which results in the discovery of a child whom he has never met. Dr. Klein suggests that he spend a weekend with the kid, so in walks teen-aged Angela. Reluctant to develop his role as a father, Roy also gets heavily involved in Frank's ambitious swindle.Matchstick Men is about con artists.But the story is more about the characters involved in the story rather than the con that these characters get involved with.It was a character-driven story that has many interesting subplots and as the film progresses,it gets better and better.It provides an intriguing exploration of dishonesty in its varied forms.Also,it the performance of the cast was great especially with Cage,who is absolutely terrific down to his eye twitches and neck jerks as a phobic con artist.Special mention should also go to the 23- year-old Alison Loman,who played the role of the 14-year-old Angela.The humor was also funny.And most importantly,the story was never predictable and formulaic from beginning to end.Matchstick Men is a brilliant character-study and enjoyable film that will appeal to anyone who loves good movies.It is close to being a masterpiece.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Too much Cage not enough Rockwell</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There are very few films that interact with the audience the way that Ridley Scott's Matchstick Men does. It opens as a film about con men, turns into a film about family men, and ends one-hundred and eighty degrees from where it began. Along the way Scott plays the audience in his own kind of con, and he pulls it off pretty well.Nicolas Cage plays Roy an obsessive compulsive grifter, who at the urging of his partner Frank, played by Sam Rockwell, visits a shrink who directs him to the now teenage daughter he left with his ex-wife. While this is happening Roy and Frank are involved in a con against a rich business man. That is all that can be said about the story without giving anything away.The driving force behind Matchstick Men is Nicolas Cage and his wacky acting. At times he is over the top, but for the most part he turns in a good performance. Unfortunately for Cage the supporting acting is good enough to make him look like he is trying to hard. Were Matchstick Men fails isn't in the story, which is confusing but for a reason and works in self out in the end, but in its failure to utilize the talents of Sam Rockwell. Rockwell is brilliant, but he isn't on screen enough to make people take notice. If Ridley Scott were to remake this film, he could greatly improve on it by adding more scenes with Sam Rockwell. Overall Matchstick Men succeeds and frustrates at the same time.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2004-09-23</date>
		<username>Dan</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Fine effort wrecked by clichéd violence</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Another example of tremendous effort put into a film whose screenplay exhibits that most common of early 21st century screenplay weaknesses, the inability to resist the temptation to substitute violent plot turns for real character development. All of the actors here turn in fine performances (and that's saying something, because I'm not generally a Cage fan). Some of the whimsy is a shade over the top, (the scene of the cowboy-hat-wearing sidekick casing the lobby of a high-rise office building, for instance), but overall the first part of the movie works. But the whimsical tone of the movie is shattered with a gruesome, unexpected, and disturbingly realistic moment of violence, complete with a look of shock and horror on the victim's face, plenty of blood, a death rattle, and limb-twitching. I guess I can only sigh and conclude that I'm the only moviegoer left on the planet who does not appreciate movies that feel they have to rip my nerves raw with a graphic murder to get a reaction from me. Let's not build dramatic tension slowly, and break through to a genuine climax; let's just pull out a gun and blow a hole through somebody to jump-start the plot. That shows how edgy we are. That shows how bold we are. That shows how lazy, and more important, clichéd we are.I can't say a lot about how the movie progressed after the bloodletting because I soon got up and walked out. But I saw enough to say that the violent plot twist had its usual effect: Suddenly the characters are no longer believable. The sweet, sheltered 14-year-old daughter is far more concerned with the fact that her new-found daddy no longer wants to see her, than by the murder. That doesn't make sense. Oh, I suppose the writers could pawn it off by saying, "EXACTLY! Moment of violence, and suddenly, NOTHING MAKES SENSE! Get it?" Well then, why not just cut to a shot of her giving her inaugural address after being elected the first 14-year-old female president? Or maybe piloting an F-16, single-handedly destroying a fleet of alien invaders? Or leaping through the air in slo-mo, ala Antonio Banderas, shooting two guns in opposite directions?Because, at bottom, this movie's resorts to clichés every bit as thin and worn as "Independence Day" or "Once Upon a Time in Mexico". Yet it was clearly aiming at something far higher. Hence my disappointment.</fullReview>
	</review>
